<p><b>Aachen</b> (<small>German pronunciation:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈʔaːxən]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Aachen-2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7d/De-Aachen-2.ogg" class="internal" title="De-Aachen-2.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>) or <b>Bad Aachen</b>, French and traditional English: <b>Aix-la-Chapelle</b> (<small>French pronunciation:&#160;​</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[ˌɛkslaʃaˈpɛl]</a></span>), is a <a href="/wiki/Spa_town" title="Spa town">spa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Border_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Border city">border city</a><sup id="cite_ref-young_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-young-2">[2]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">North Rhine-Westphalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. Aachen developed from a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> settlement and spa, subsequently becoming the preferred medieval Imperial residence of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and, from 936 to 1531, the place where 31 <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Emperors</a> were crowned <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_monarchs" title="List of German monarchs">Kings of the Germans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-munro_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-munro-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aachen is the westernmost city in Germany, located near the borders with <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, 61&#160;km (38&#160;mi) west south west of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a><sup id="cite_ref-CVDE_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EA_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-6">[6]</a></sup> in a former coal-mining area.<sup id="cite_ref-EA_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-6">[6]</a></sup> One of Germany's leading institutes of higher education in technology, the <a href="/wiki/RWTH_Aachen_University" title="RWTH Aachen University">RWTH Aachen University</a> is located in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[a]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rwth-aachen.de_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwth-aachen.de-8">[7]</a></sup> Aachen's industries include science, engineering and information technology. In 2009, Aachen was ranked eighth among cities in Germany for innovation.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name "Aachen" is a modern descendant, like southern German <i>Ach(e)</i>, <i>Aach</i>, meaning "river" or "stream", from <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>ahha</i>, meaning "water" or "stream", which directly translates (and etymologically corresponds to) <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>Aquae</i>, referring to the springs. The location has been inhabited by humans since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> era, about 5,000 years ago, attracted to its warm <a href="/wiki/Mineral_spring" title="Mineral spring">mineral springs</a>. Latin <i>Aquae</i> figures in Aachen's <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> name <i>Aquae granni</i>, which meant "waters of <a href="/wiki/Grannus" title="Grannus">Grannus</a>", referring to the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> god of healing who was worshipped at the springs.<sup id="cite_ref-munro_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-munro-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tourism_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tourism-10">[9]</a></sup> This word became <i>Åxhe</i> in <a href="/wiki/Walloon_language" title="Walloon language">Walloon</a> and <i>Aix</i> in French, and subsequently <i>Aix-la-Chapelle</i> after <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> had his <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Chapel,_Aachen" title="Palatine Chapel, Aachen">palatine chapel</a> built there in the late eighth century and then made the city his empire's capital.</p>
<p>Aachen's name in French and German evolved in parallel. The city is known by a variety of different names in other languages:</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aachen_dialect">Aachen dialect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Aachen dialect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aachen is at the western end of the <a href="/wiki/Benrath_line" title="Benrath line">Benrath line</a> that divides <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">High German</a> to the south from the rest of the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> speech area to the north.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Aachen's local dialect is called <i><a href="/wiki/Aachen_dialect" title="Aachen dialect">Öcher Platt</a></i> and belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_language" title="Ripuarian language">Ripuarian language</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Free_Imperial_City_of_Aachen" title="Free Imperial City of Aachen">Free Imperial City of Aachen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Aachen" title="Timeline of Aachen">Timeline of Aachen</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Flint quarries on the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lousberg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lousberg (page does not exist)">Lousberg</a>, Schneeberg, and Königshügel, first used during <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> times (3000–2500 BC), attest to the long occupation of the site of Aachen, as do recent finds under the modern city's <i>Elisengarten</i> pointing to a former settlement from the same period. <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (around 1600 BC) settlement is evidenced by the remains of barrows (burial mounds) found, for example, on the Klausberg. During the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, the area was settled by Celtic peoples<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> who were perhaps drawn by the marshy Aachen basin's <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot sulphur springs</a> where they worshipped <a href="/wiki/Grannus" title="Grannus">Grannus</a>, god of light and healing.</p>
<p>Later, the 25-hectare Roman spa resort town of Aquae Granni was, according to legend, founded by Grenus, under <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>, around AD 124. Instead, the fictitious founder refers to the Celtic god, and it seems it was the Roman 6th Legion at the start of the 1st century AD that first channelled the hot springs into a spa at Büchel,<sup id="cite_ref-CVDE_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[b]</a></sup> adding at the end of the same century the <i>Münstertherme</i> spa,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> two water pipelines, and a probable<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> sanctuary dedicated to Grannus. A kind of forum, surrounded by colonnades, connected the two spa complexes. There was also an extensive residential area, part of it inhabited by a flourishing Jewish community.<sup id="cite_ref-jewishencyclopedia.com_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jewishencyclopedia.com-18">[16]</a></sup> The Romans built bathhouses near <a href="/wiki/Burtscheid" title="Burtscheid">Burtscheid</a>. A temple precinct called <i>Vernenum</i> was built near the modern <a href="/wiki/Kornelim%C3%BCnster/Walheim" title="Kornelimünster/Walheim">Kornelimünster/Walheim</a>. Today, remains have been found of three bathhouses,<sup id="cite_ref-DofA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA-19">[17]</a></sup> including two fountains in the <i>Elisenbrunnen</i> and the Burtscheid bathhouse.</p>
<p>Roman civil administration in Aachen broke down between the end of the 4th and beginning of the 5th centuries. Rome withdrew its troops from the area, but the town remained populated. By 470, the town came to be ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_Franks" title="Ripuarian Franks">Ripuarian Franks</a><sup id="cite_ref-Coll_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coll-20">[18]</a></sup> and subordinated to their capital, <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg/220px-Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg/330px-Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg/440px-Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1059" data-file-height="1515" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachener_Heiligtumszeigung_Oelgemaelde_17Jh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Presentation of the four "Great Relics" during the Aachen pilgrimage, after a 17th-century painting</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Roman times, <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a> had a castle residence built in the town, due to the proximity of the hot springs and also for strategic reasons as it is located between the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> and northern France.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA2-21">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Einhard" title="Einhard">Einhard</a> mentions that in 765–6 Pepin spent both Christmas and Easter at <i>Aquis villa</i> (<i><span lang="la">Et celebravit natalem Domini in Aquis villa et pascha similiter.</span></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> ("and [he] celebrated Christmas in the town Aquis, and similarly Easter") which must have been sufficiently equipped to support the royal household for several months. In the year of his coronation as king of the <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Franks</a>, 768, <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> came to spend Christmas at Aachen for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[c]</a></sup> He remained there in a mansion which he may have extended, although there is no source attesting to any significant building activity at Aachen in his time, apart from the building of the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Chapel_in_Aachen" class="mw-redirect" title="Palatine Chapel in Aachen">Palatine Chapel</a> (since 1930, cathedral) and the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Aachen" title="Palace of Aachen">Palace</a>. Charlemagne spent most winters in Aachen between 792 and his death in 814. Aachen became the focus of his court and the political centre of his empire. After his death, the king was buried in the church which he had built;<sup id="cite_ref-DofA5_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA5-25">[22]</a></sup> his original tomb has been lost, while his alleged remains are preserved in the <i><a href="/wiki/Karlsschrein" title="Karlsschrein">Karlsschrein</a></i>, the shrine where he was reburied after being declared a saint; his saintliness, however, was never officially acknowledged by the Roman Curia as such.</p>
<p>In 936, <a href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto I</a> was crowned king of <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a> in the collegiate church built by Charlemagne. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Otto_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto II">Otto II</a>, the nobles revolted and the <a href="/wiki/West_Francia" title="West Francia">West Franks</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Lothair_of_France" title="Lothair of France">Lothair</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-dupuy_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dupuy-26">[23]</a></sup> raided Aachen in the ensuing confusion.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[d]</a></sup> Aachen was attacked again by <a href="/wiki/Odo_II,_Count_of_Blois" title="Odo II, Count of Blois">Odo of Champagne</a>, who attacked the imperial palace while <a href="/wiki/Conrad_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Conrad II, Holy Roman Emperor">Conrad II</a> was absent. Odo relinquished it quickly and was killed soon afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> The palace and town of Aachen had fortifying walls built by order of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick Barbarossa</a> between 1172 and 1176.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA-19">[17]</a></sup> Over the next 500 years, most kings of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Germany</a> destined to reign over the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> were crowned in Aachen. The original audience hall built by Charlemagne was torn down and replaced by the current <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Rathaus" title="Aachen Rathaus">city hall</a> in 1330.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[e]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DofA_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA-19">[17]</a></sup> The last king to be crowned here was <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand I, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand I</a> in 1531.<sup id="cite_ref-CVDE_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AAE_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAE-31">[26]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Aachen remained a city of regional importance, due to its proximity to <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>; it achieved a modest position in the trade in <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">woollen</a> cloths, favoured by imperial privilege. The city remained a <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">free imperial city</a>, subject to the emperor only, but was politically far too weak to influence the policies of any of its neighbours. The only dominion it had was over <a href="/wiki/Burtscheid" title="Burtscheid">Burtscheid</a>, a neighbouring territory ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> <a href="/wiki/Abbess" title="Abbess">abbess</a>. It was forced to accept that all of its traffic must pass through the "Aachener Reich". Even in the late 18th century the Abbess of Burtscheid was prevented from building a road linking her territory to the neighbouring estates of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_J%C3%BClich" title="Duchy of Jülich">duke of Jülich</a>; the city of Aachen even deployed its handful of soldiers to chase away the road-diggers.</p>
<p>As an imperial city, Aachen held certain political privileges that allowed it to remain independent<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the troubles of Europe for many years. It remained a direct vassal of the Holy Roman Empire throughout most of the Middle Ages. It was also the site of many important church councils, including the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Council_of_837&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Council of 837 (page does not exist)">Council of 837</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Council_of_1166&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Council of 1166 (page does not exist)">Council of 1166</a>, a council convened by the <a href="/wiki/Antipope" title="Antipope">antipope</a> <a href="/wiki/Paschal_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Paschal III">Paschal III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EA_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuscript_production">Manuscript production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Manuscript production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aachen has proved an important site for the production of historical manuscripts. Under Charlemagne's purview, both the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Gospels_(Ada_School)" title="Aachen Gospels (Ada School)">Ada Gospels</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_Gospel" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation Gospel">Coronation Gospels</a> may have been produced in Aachen.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA1-33">[28]</a></sup> In addition, quantities of the other texts in the court library were also produced locally. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a> (814–840), substantial quantities of ancient texts were produced at Aachen, including legal manuscripts such as the leges scriptorium group, <a href="/wiki/Patristic" class="mw-redirect" title="Patristic">patristic</a> texts including the five manuscripts of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bamberg_Pliny_Group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bamberg Pliny Group (page does not exist)">Bamberg Pliny Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA1_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA1-33">[28]</a></sup> Finally, under <a href="/wiki/Lothair_I" title="Lothair I">Lothair I</a> (840–855), texts of outstanding quality were still being produced. This however marked the end of the period of manuscript production at Aachen.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA1_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA1-33">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="16th–18th_centuries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="16th.E2.80.9318th_centuries">16th–18th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 16th–18th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Siege_of_Aachen.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/The_Siege_of_Aachen.png/260px-The_Siege_of_Aachen.png" width="260" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/The_Siege_of_Aachen.png/390px-The_Siege_of_Aachen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/The_Siege_of_Aachen.png/520px-The_Siege_of_Aachen.png 2x" data-file-width="1119" data-file-height="803" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Siege_of_Aachen.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Aachen_(1614)" title="Siege of Aachen (1614)">siege of Aachen</a> by the Spanish Army of Flanders under Ambrogio Spinola in 1614</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg/260px-Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg" width="260" height="84" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg/390px-Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg/520px-Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1729" data-file-height="560" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhein-Strom_0184a_Achen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of Aachen in 1690</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1598, following the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a> troops from the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Rudolf II, Holy Roman Emperor">Rudolf</a> deposed all Protestant office holders in Aachen and even went as far as expelling them from the city.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup> From the early 16th century, Aachen started to lose its power and influence. First the coronations of emperors were moved from Aachen to <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>. This was followed by the religious wars, and the great fire of 1656.<sup id="cite_ref-eb_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eb-35">[30]</a></sup> After the destruction of most of the city in 1656, the rebuilding was mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> style.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA-19">[17]</a></sup> The decline of Aachen culminated in 1794, when the French, led by General <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dumouriez" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Dumouriez">Charles Dumouriez</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Coll_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coll-20">[18]</a></sup> occupied Aachen.<sup id="cite_ref-AAE_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAE-31">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the middle of the 17th century Aachen had become attractive as a spa: not so much because of the effects of the hot springs on the health of its visitors but because Aachen was then – and remained well into the 19th century – a place of high-level prostitution.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Traces of this hidden agenda of the city's history are found in the 18th-century guidebooks to Aachen as well as to the other spas.</p>
<p>The main <a href="/wiki/Indication_(medicine)" title="Indication (medicine)">indication</a> for visiting patients, ironically, was <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>; only by the end of the 19th century had rheumatism become the most important object of cures at Aachen and Burtscheid.</p>
<p>Aachen was chosen as the site of several important congresses and peace treaties: the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1668)" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle (1668)">first congress of Aachen</a> (often referred to as the Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle in English) on 2 May 1668,<sup id="cite_ref-dupuy1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dupuy1-36">[31]</a></sup> leading to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1668)" title="Treaty of Aix-la-Chapelle (1668)">First Treaty of Aachen</a> in the same year which ended the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Devolution" title="War of Devolution">War of Devolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1748)" title="Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle (1748)">second congress</a> ended with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1748)" title="Treaty of Aix-la-Chapelle (1748)">second treaty</a> in 1748, ending the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Austrian_Succession" title="War of the Austrian Succession">War of the Austrian Succession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CVDE_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> In 1789, there was a constitutional crisis in the Aachen government,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup> and in 1794 Aachen lost its status as a <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">free imperial city</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA-19">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 9 February 1801, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Lun%C3%A9ville" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace of Lunéville">Peace of Lunéville</a> removed the ownership of Aachen and the entire "left bank" of the Rhine from Germany and granted it to France.<sup id="cite_ref-Coll_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coll-20">[18]</a></sup> In 1815, control of the town was passed to <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, by an act passed by the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA-19">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AAE_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAE-31">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1818)" title="Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle (1818)">third congress</a> took place in 1818, to decide the fate of occupied <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleonic</a> France.</p>
<p>By the middle of the 19th century, industrialisation had swept away most of the city's medieval rules of production and commerce, although the entirely corrupt<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> remains of the city's medieval constitution were kept in place (compare the famous remarks of Georg Forster in his <i>Ansichten vom Niederrhein</i>) until 1801, when Aachen became the "<a href="/wiki/Prefectures_in_France" title="Prefectures in France">chef-lieu</a> du <a href="/wiki/Roer_(department)" title="Roer (department)">département de la Roer</a>" in Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a>. In 1815, after the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a> took over. The city was one of its most socially and politically backward centres until the end of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-CVDE_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-5">[5]</a></sup> Administered within the <a href="/wiki/Rhine_Province" title="Rhine Province">Rhine Province</a>, by 1880 the population was 80,000. Starting in 1838, the railway from <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> to <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> passed through Aachen.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> The city suffered extreme overcrowding and deplorable sanitary conditions until 1875, when the medieval fortifications were finally abandoned as a limit to building and new, better housing was built in the east of the city, where sanitary drainage was easiest. In December 1880, the <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Aachen" title="Trams in Aachen">Aachen tramway network</a> was opened, and in 1895 it was electrified.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> In the 19th century and up to the 1930s, the city was important in the production of railway locomotives and carriages, iron, <a href="/wiki/Pin" title="Pin">pins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sewing_needle" title="Sewing needle">needles</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing_button&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Clothing button (page does not exist)">buttons</a>, tobacco, woollen goods, and silk goods.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG/220px-KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG/330px-KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG/440px-KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KleineSchwimmhalle.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The modern Elisabethhalle pool</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Aachen was occupied by the Allies until 1930, along with the rest of German territory west of the Rhine.<sup id="cite_ref-AAE_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAE-31">[26]</a></sup> Aachen was one of the locations involved in the ill-fated <a href="/wiki/Rhenish_Republic" title="Rhenish Republic">Rhenish Republic</a>. On 21 October 1923, an armed band took over the city hall. Similar actions took place in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6nchengladbach" title="Mönchengladbach">Mönchen-Gladbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duisburg" title="Duisburg">Duisburg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Krefeld" title="Krefeld">Krefeld</a>. This republic lasted only about a year.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> Aachen was heavily damaged during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The city and its fortified surroundings were laid siege to from 12 September to 21 October 1944 by the US 1st Infantry Division<sup id="cite_ref-1d_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1d-43">[38]</a></sup> with the 3rd Armored Division assisting from the south.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup> Around 13 October the US 2nd Armored Division played their part, coming from the north and getting as close as <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrselen" title="Würselen">Würselen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup> while the 30th Infantry Division played a crucial role in completing the encirclement of Aachen on 16 October 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> With reinforcements from the US 28th Infantry Division<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aachen" title="Battle of Aachen">Battle of Aachen</a> then continued involving direct assaults through the heavily defended city, which finally forced the German garrison to surrender on 21 October 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-1d_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1d-43">[38]</a></sup> Aachen was the first German city to be captured by the Allies, and its residents welcomed the soldiers as liberators.<sup id="cite_ref-baker2004_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baker2004-48">[43]</a></sup> The city was destroyed partially – and in some parts completely – during the fighting,<sup id="cite_ref-CVDE_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CVDE-5">[5]</a></sup> mostly by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">American</a> <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> fire and demolitions carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a> defenders. Damaged buildings included the medieval churches of <a href="/wiki/Foillan" title="Foillan">St. Foillan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">St. Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Nicholas" title="Saint Nicholas">St. Nicholas</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Rathaus" title="Aachen Rathaus">Rathaus</a> (city hall), although <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Cathedral" title="Aachen Cathedral">Aachen Cathedral</a> was largely unscathed. Only 4,000 inhabitants remained in the city; the rest had followed evacuation orders. Its first Allied-appointed mayor, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Oppenhoff" title="Franz Oppenhoff">Franz Oppenhoff</a>, was assassinated by an SS commando unit.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Aachen_Jews">History of Aachen Jews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History of Aachen Jews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> period, Aachen was the site of a flourishing <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> community. Later, during the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian" class="mw-redirect" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> empire, a Jewish community lived near the royal palace.<sup id="cite_ref-jewishencyclopedia.com_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jewishencyclopedia.com-18">[16]</a></sup> In 802, a Jew named Isaac accompanied the ambassador of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> to <a href="/wiki/Harun_al-Rashid" title="Harun al-Rashid">Harun al-Rashid</a>. During the 13th century, many Jews converted to Christianity, as shown in the records of the Aachen Minster (today's cathedral). In 1486, the Jews of Aachen offered gifts to <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a> during his coronation ceremony. In 1629, the Aachen Jewish community was expelled from the city. In 1667, six Jews were allowed to return. Most of the Aachen Jews settled in the nearby town of <a href="/wiki/Burtscheid" title="Burtscheid">Burtscheid</a>. On 16 May 1815, the Jewish community of the city offered an homage in its synagogue to the Prussian king, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Friedrich Wilhelm III">Friedrich Wilhelm III</a>. A Jewish cemetery was acquired in 1851. 1,345 Jews lived in the city in 1933. The synagogue was destroyed during <a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">Kristallnacht</a> in 1938. In 1939, after emigration and arrests, 782 Jews remained in the city. After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, only 62 Jews lived there. In 2003, 1,434 Jews were living in Aachen. In Jewish texts, the city of Aachen was called Aish, or Ash (אש).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city of Aachen has developed into a technology hub as a by-product of hosting one of the leading <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Technology">universities of technology</a> in Germany with the <a href="/wiki/RWTH_Aachen" class="mw-redirect" title="RWTH Aachen">RWTH Aachen</a> (Rheinisch-Westfälische Technische Hochschule), known especially for mechanical engineering, automotive and manufacturing technology as well as for its research and academic hospital <a href="/wiki/Klinikum_Aachen" class="mw-redirect" title="Klinikum Aachen">Klinikum Aachen</a>, one of the largest medical facilities in Europe.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drilandenpunt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Drilandenpunt.jpg/200px-Drilandenpunt.jpg" width="200" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Drilandenpunt.jpg/300px-Drilandenpunt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Drilandenpunt.jpg/400px-Drilandenpunt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1028" data-file-height="1710" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drilandenpunt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The three-country point, where the borders of Germany, Belgium, and the Netherlands meet</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aachen is located in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Meuse%E2%80%93Rhine_Euroregion" title="Meuse–Rhine Euroregion">Meuse–Rhine Euroregion</a>, close to the border <a href="/wiki/Tripoint" title="Tripoint">tripoint</a> of Germany, the Netherlands, and Belgium. The city of <a href="/wiki/Heerlen" title="Heerlen">Heerlen</a> in the Netherlands lies nearby, as does <a href="/wiki/Eupen" title="Eupen">Eupen</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/German-speaking_Community_of_Belgium" title="German-speaking Community of Belgium">German-speaking Community of Belgium</a>, both located about 20&#160;km (12.4&#160;mi) from Aachen's city centre. Aachen lies near the head of the open valley of the <a href="/wiki/Wurm" title="Wurm">Wurm</a> (which today flows through the city in canalised form), part of the larger basin of the <a href="/wiki/Meuse" title="Meuse">Meuse</a>, and about 30&#160;km (19&#160;mi) north of the <a href="/wiki/High_Fens" title="High Fens">High Fens</a>, which form the northern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Eifel" title="Eifel">Eifel</a> uplands of the <a href="/wiki/Rhenish_Massif" title="Rhenish Massif">Rhenish Massif</a>.</p>
<p>The maximum dimensions of the city's territory are 21.6&#160;km (13.4&#160;mi) from north to south, and 17.2&#160;km (10.7&#160;mi) from east to west. The city limits are 87.7&#160;km (54.5&#160;mi) long, of which 23.8&#160;km (14.8&#160;mi) border Belgium and 21.8&#160;km (13.5&#160;mi) the Netherlands. The highest point in Aachen, located in the far southeast of the city, lies at an elevation of 410&#160;m above sea level. The lowest point, in the north, and on the border with the Netherlands, is at 125&#160;m.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the westernmost city in Germany<sup id="cite_ref-munro_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-munro-4">[4]</a></sup> (and close to the Low Countries), Aachen and the surrounding area belongs to a <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate climate zone</a>, with humid weather, mild winters, and warm summers. Because of its location north of the <a href="/wiki/Eifel" title="Eifel">Eifel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/High_Fens" title="High Fens">High Fens</a> and its subsequent prevailing westerly weather patterns, rainfall in Aachen (on average 805&#160;mm/year) is comparatively higher than, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> (with 669&#160;mm/year). Another factor in the local weather forces of Aachen is the occurrence of <a href="/wiki/Foehn_winds" class="mw-redirect" title="Foehn winds">Foehn winds</a> on the southerly air currents, which results from the city's geographic location on the northern edge of the Eifel.<sup id="cite_ref-climate_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climate-49">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because the city is surrounded by hills, it suffers from <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(meteorology)" title="Inversion (meteorology)">inversion-related</a> smog. Some areas of the city have become <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">urban heat islands</a> as a result of poor heat exchange, both because of the area's natural geography and from human activity. The city's numerous cold air corridors, which are slated to remain as free as possible from new construction, therefore play an important role in the urban climate of Aachen.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The January average is 3.0&#160;°C (37&#160;°F), while the July average is 18.5&#160;°C (65&#160;°F). Precipitation is almost evenly spread throughout the year.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG/220px-Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG/330px-Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG/440px-Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1243" data-file-height="914" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Felsen_St.Adalbert.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Layered <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claystone" class="mw-redirect" title="Claystone">claystone</a> <a href="/wiki/Formation_(stratigraphy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Formation (stratigraphy)">formation</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period below St. Adalbert Church in Aachen</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The geology of Aachen is very structurally heterogeneous. The oldest occurring rocks in the area surrounding the city originate from the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period and include <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">carboniferous</a> <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greywacke" title="Greywacke">greywacke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claystone" class="mw-redirect" title="Claystone">claystone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>. These formations are part of the <a href="/wiki/Rhenish_Massif" title="Rhenish Massif">Rhenish Massif</a>, north of the High Fens. In the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvanian_(geology)" title="Pennsylvanian (geology)">Pennsylvanian</a> subperiod of the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> geological period, these rock layers were narrowed and folded as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Variscan_orogeny" title="Variscan orogeny">Variscan orogeny</a>. After this event, and over the course of the following 200 million years, this area has been continuously flattened.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period, the ocean penetrated the continent from the direction of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> up to the mountainous area near Aachen, bringing with it clay, sand, and chalk deposits. While the clay (which was the basis for a major <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> industry in nearby <a href="/wiki/Raeren" title="Raeren">Raeren</a>) is mostly found in the lower areas of Aachen, the hills of the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Forest" title="Aachen Forest">Aachen Forest</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lousberg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lousberg (page does not exist)">Lousberg</a> were formed from upper Cretaceous sand and chalk deposits. More recent sedimentation is mainly located in the north and east of Aachen and was formed through <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">tertiary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">quaternary</a> river and wind activities.</p>
<p>Along the major <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrust fault</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Variscan_orogeny" title="Variscan orogeny">Variscan orogeny</a>, there are over 30 <a href="/wiki/Thermal_spring" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal spring">thermal springs</a> in Aachen and <a href="/wiki/Burtscheid" title="Burtscheid">Burtscheid</a>. Additionally, the subsurface of Aachen is traversed by numerous <a href="/wiki/Active_fault" title="Active fault">active faults</a> that belong to the Rurgraben fault system, which has, in the past, been responsible for numerous earthquakes, including the 1756 <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCren" title="Düren">Düren</a> earthquake<sup id="cite_ref-duerenEQ_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duerenEQ-53">[48]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/1992_Roermond_earthquake" title="1992 Roermond earthquake">1992 Roermond earthquake</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Roermond_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roermond-54">[49]</a></sup> which was the strongest earthquake ever recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg/220px-Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg.png" width="220" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg/330px-Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg/440px-Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Age_structure_aachen_vs_germany.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Age distribution of Aachen's population next to Germany's (2014)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aachen has 245,885 inhabitants (as of 31 December 2015), of whom 118,272 are female, and 127,613 are male.<sup id="cite_ref-Zensus_2011_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zensus_2011-55">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>The unemployment rate in the city is, as of April 2012, 9.7 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup> At the end of 2009, the foreign-born residents of Aachen made up 13.6 percent of the total population.<sup id="cite_ref-StatJahrbuch2009_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StatJahrbuch2009-57">[52]</a></sup> A significant portion of foreign residents are students at the <a href="/wiki/RWTH_Aachen_University" title="RWTH Aachen University">RWTH Aachen University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boroughs">Boroughs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Boroughs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city is divided into seven administrative districts, or boroughs, each with its own district council, district leader, and district authority. The councils are elected locally by those who live within the district, and these districts are further subdivided into smaller sections for statistical purposes, with each sub-district named by a two-digit number.</p>
<p>The districts of Aachen, including their constituent statistical districts, are:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aachen-Mitte" title="Aachen-Mitte">Aachen-Mitte</a>: 10 Markt, 13 Theater, 14 Lindenplatz, 15 St. Jakob, 16 Westpark, 17 Hanbruch, 18 Hörn, 21 <a href="/wiki/Ponttor" title="Ponttor">Ponttor</a>, 22 Hansemannplatz, 23 <a href="/wiki/Soers" title="Soers">Soers</a>, 24 Jülicher Straße, 25 Kalkofen, 31 Kaiserplatz, 32 Adalbertsteinweg, 33 Panneschopp, 34 <a href="/wiki/Rothe_Erde" title="Rothe Erde">Rothe Erde</a>, 35 Trierer Straße, 36 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Frankenberger_Viertel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Frankenberger Viertel (page does not exist)">Frankenberg</a>, 37 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Forst_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Forst (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Forst</a>, 41 Beverau, 42 <a href="/wiki/Burtscheid" title="Burtscheid">Burtscheid</a> Kurgarten, 43 Burtscheid Abbey, 46 Burtscheid Steinebrück, 47 Marschiertor, 48 Hangeweiher</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brand_(Aachen)" title="Brand (Aachen)">Brand</a>: 51 Brand</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eilendorf_(Aachen)" title="Eilendorf (Aachen)">Eilendorf</a>: 52 Eilendorf</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haaren_(Aachen)" title="Haaren (Aachen)">Haaren</a>: 53 Haaren (including <a href="/wiki/Verlautenheide" title="Verlautenheide">Verlautenheide</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kornelim%C3%BCnster/Walheim" title="Kornelimünster/Walheim">Kornelimünster/Walheim</a>: 61 <a href="/wiki/Kornelim%C3%BCnster" title="Kornelimünster">Kornelimünster</a>, 62 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Oberforstbach&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Oberforstbach (page does not exist)">Oberforstbach</a>, 63 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Walheim_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Walheim (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Walheim</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laurensberg" title="Laurensberg">Laurensberg</a>: 64 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vaalserquartier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vaalserquartier (page does not exist)">Vaalserquartier</a>, 65 Laurensberg</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Richterich" title="Richterich">Richterich</a>: 88 Richterich</li>
</ul>
<p>Regardless of official statistical designations, there are 50 neighbourhoods and communities within Aachen, here arranged by district:</p>
<ul>
<li>Aachen-Mitte: Beverau, Bildchen, <a href="/wiki/Burtscheid" title="Burtscheid">Burtscheid</a>, Forst, Frankenberg, Grüne Eiche, Hörn, Lintert, <a href="/wiki/Ponttor" title="Ponttor">Pontviertel</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Preuswald&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Preuswald (page does not exist)">Preuswald</a>, Ronheide, Rosviertel, Rothe Erde, Stadtmitte, Steinebrück, West</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brand_(Aachen)" title="Brand (Aachen)">Brand</a>: Brand, Eich, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Freund_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Freund (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Freund</a>, Hitfeld, Niederforstbach</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eilendorf_(Aachen)" title="Eilendorf (Aachen)">Eilendorf</a>: Eilendorf, Nirm</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haaren_(Aachen)" title="Haaren (Aachen)">Haaren</a>: Haaren, Hüls, Verlautenheide</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kornelim%C3%BCnster/Walheim" title="Kornelimünster/Walheim">Kornelimünster/Walheim</a>: <a href="/wiki/Friesenrath" title="Friesenrath">Friesenrath</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hahn_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hahn (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Hahn</a>, Kitzenhaus, Kornelimünster, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Krauthausen_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Krauthausen (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Krauthausen</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lichtenbusch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lichtenbusch (page does not exist)">Lichtenbusch</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=N%C3%BCtheim&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nütheim (page does not exist)">Nütheim</a>, Oberforstbach, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sief&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sief (page does not exist)">Sief</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Schleckheim&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Schleckheim (page does not exist)">Schleckheim</a>, Schmithof, Walheim</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laurensberg" title="Laurensberg">Laurensberg</a>: Gut Kullen, Kronenberg, Laurensberg, <a href="/wiki/Lemiers" title="Lemiers">Lemiers</a>, Melaten, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Orsbach&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Orsbach (page does not exist)">Orsbach</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Seffent&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Seffent (page does not exist)">Seffent</a>, Soers, Steppenberg, Vaalserquartier, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vetschau_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vetschau (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Vetschau</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Richterich" title="Richterich">Richterich</a>: <a href="/w/index.php?title=Horbach_(Aachen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Horbach (Aachen) (page does not exist)">Horbach</a>, Huf, Richterich</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neighbouring_communities">Neighbouring communities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Neighbouring communities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following cities and communities border Aachen, clockwise from the northwest: <a href="/wiki/Herzogenrath" title="Herzogenrath">Herzogenrath</a>, <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrselen" title="Würselen">Würselen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eschweiler" title="Eschweiler">Eschweiler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stolberg_(Rhineland)" title="Stolberg (Rhineland)">Stolberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roetgen" title="Roetgen">Roetgen</a> (which are all in the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_(district)" title="Aachen (district)">district of Aachen</a>); <a href="/wiki/Raeren" title="Raeren">Raeren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kelmis" title="Kelmis">Kelmis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plombi%C3%A8res" title="Plombières">Plombières</a> (<a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge_(province)" title="Liège (province)">Lüttich Province</a> in Belgium) as well as <a href="/wiki/Vaals" title="Vaals">Vaals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulpen-Wittem" title="Gulpen-Wittem">Gulpen-Wittem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simpelveld" title="Simpelveld">Simpelveld</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heerlen" title="Heerlen">Heerlen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerkrade" title="Kerkrade">Kerkrade</a> (all in <a href="/wiki/Limburg_(Netherlands)" title="Limburg (Netherlands)">Limburg Province</a> in the Netherlands).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_sights">Main sights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Main sights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aachen_Cathedral">Aachen Cathedral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aachen Cathedral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg/260px-Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg" width="260" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg/390px-Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg/520px-Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4690" data-file-height="3350" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Germany_Imperial-Cathedral-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aachen Cathedral</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aachen_Cathedral" title="Aachen Cathedral">Aachen Cathedral</a> was erected on the orders of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> in AD 796<sup id="cite_ref-EA_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-6">[6]</a></sup> and was, on completion, in 798,<sup id="cite_ref-DofA3_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA3-60">[55]</a></sup> the largest cathedral north of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>. It was modelled after the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, Italy,<sup id="cite_ref-AAE_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAE-31">[26]</a></sup> and was built by <a href="/wiki/Odo_of_Metz" title="Odo of Metz">Odo of Metz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EA_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-6">[6]</a></sup> Charlemagne also desired for the chapel to compete with the <a href="/wiki/Lateran_Palace" title="Lateran Palace">Lateran Palace</a>, both in quality and authority.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> It was originally built in the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_architecture" title="Carolingian architecture">Carolingian</a> style, including marble covered walls, and mosaic inlay on the dome.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA4_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA4-62">[57]</a></sup> On his death, Charlemagne's remains were interred in the cathedral and can be seen there to this day. The cathedral was extended several times in later ages, turning it into a curious and unique mixture of building styles. The throne and gallery portion date from the <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_dynasty" title="Ottonian dynasty">Ottonian</a>, with portions of the original <a href="/wiki/Opus_sectile" title="Opus sectile">opus sectile</a> floor still visible.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA4_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA4-62">[57]</a></sup> The 13th century saw gables being added to the roof, and after the fire of 1656, the dome was rebuilt. Finally, a <a href="/wiki/Choir_(architecture)" title="Choir (architecture)">choir</a> was added around the start of the 15th century<sup id="cite_ref-DofA5_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA5-25">[22]</a></sup> After, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Barbarossa" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Barbarossa">Frederick Barbarossa</a> <a href="/wiki/Canonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonisation">canonised</a> Charlemagne, in 1165, the chapel became a destination for pilgrims.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA5_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA5-25">[22]</a></sup> For 600 years, from 936 to 1531, Aachen Cathedral was the church of coronation for 30 German kings and 12 queens. The church built by Charlemagne is still the main attraction of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup> In addition to holding the remains of its founder, it became the burial place of his successor <a href="/wiki/Otto_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto III, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto III</a>. In the upper chamber of the gallery, Charlemagne's marble throne is housed.<sup id="cite_ref-young2_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-young2-64">[59]</a></sup> Aachen Cathedral has been designated as a UNESCO <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of the marble and columns used in the construction of the cathedral were brought from Rome and <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> that Charlemagne was eventually laid to rest in.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> A bronze bear from <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> was placed inside, along with an equestrian statue from Ravenna, believed to be <a href="/wiki/Theodric" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodric">Theodric</a>. These were in contrast to a wolf and a statue of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitoline">Capitoline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> Bronze pieces such as the doors and railings were cast in a local foundry, some of which have survived to the present. Finally, there is uncertainty surrounding the bronze pine cone in the chapel, and where it was created. Wherever it was made, it was also a parallel to a piece in Rome, this in <a href="/wiki/Old_St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="Old St. Peter's Basilica">Old St. Peter's Basilica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cathedral_Treasury">Cathedral Treasury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cathedral Treasury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg/170px-Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg/255px-Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg/340px-Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1891" data-file-height="2834" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Germany_Domschatz_Cross-of-Lothair-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Lothair" title="Cross of Lothair">Cross of Lothair</a>, Aachen Cathedral Treasury</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aachen_Cathedral_Treasury" title="Aachen Cathedral Treasury">Aachen Cathedral Treasury</a> has housed, throughout its history, a collection of liturgical objects. The origin of this <a href="/wiki/Church_treasure" title="Church treasure">church treasure</a> is in dispute as some say Charlemagne himself endowed his chapel with the original collection, while the rest were collected over time. Others say all of the objects were collected over time, from such places as <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> The location of this treasury has moved over time and was unknown until the 15th century when it was located in the Matthiaskapelle (St. Matthew's Chapel) until 1873, when it was moved to the Karlskapelle (Charles' Chapel). From there it was moved to the Hungarian Chapel in 1881 and in 1931 to its present location next to the Allerseelenkapelle (Poor Souls' Chapel).<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> Only six of the original <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian</a> objects have remained, and of those only three are left in Aachen: the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Gospels_(Ada_School)" title="Aachen Gospels (Ada School)">Aachen Gospels</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Diptych" title="Diptych">diptych</a> of Christ, and an early <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine silk</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Coronation_Gospels" title="Coronation Gospels">Coronation Gospels</a> and a <a href="/w/index.php?title=St._Stephen%27s_Purse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="St. Stephen's Purse (page does not exist)">reliquary burse</a> of <a href="/wiki/St._Stephen" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Stephen">St. Stephen</a> were moved to <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> in 1798 and the <a href="/wiki/Talisman_of_Charlemagne" title="Talisman of Charlemagne">Talisman of Charlemagne</a> was given as a gift in 1804 to <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Josephine Bonaparte">Josephine Bonaparte</a> and subsequently to <a href="/wiki/Rheims_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Rheims Cathedral">Rheims Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> 210 documented pieces have been added to the treasury since its inception, typically to receive in return legitimisation of linkage to the heritage of Charlemagne. The <a href="/wiki/Lothar_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Lothar Cross">Lothar Cross</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gospels_of_Otto_III" title="Gospels of Otto III">Gospels of Otto III</a> and multiple additional Byzantine silks were donated by <a href="/wiki/Otto_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto III">Otto III</a>. Part of the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Cathedral#Pala_d.27Oro" title="Aachen Cathedral">Pala d'Oro</a> and a covering for the <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Gospels_(Ada_School)" title="Aachen Gospels (Ada School)">Aachen Gospels</a> were made of gold donated by <a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry II, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick Barbarossa</a> donated the candelabrum that adorns the dome and also once "crowned" the <a href="/wiki/Karlsschrein" title="Karlsschrein">Shrine of Charlemagne</a>, which was placed underneath in 1215. <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a> donated a pair of reliquaries. <a href="/wiki/Louis_XI" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XI">Louis XI</a> gave, in 1475, the crown of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_York" title="Margaret of York">Margaret of York</a>, and, in 1481, another arm reliquary of Charlemagne. <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a> both gave numerous works of art by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_von_Reutlingen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hans von Reutlingen (page does not exist)">Hans von Reutlingen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup> Continuing the tradition, objects continued to be donated until the present, each indicative of the period of its gifting, with the last documented gift being a chalice from 1960 made by <a href="/wiki/Ewald_Matar%C3%A9" title="Ewald Mataré">Ewald Mataré</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DofA6_61-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DofA6-61">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aachen_Rathaus">Aachen Rathaus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Aachen Rathaus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg/220px-AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg/330px-AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg/440px-AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4204" data-file-height="3326" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AachenCityHallFromSouthHDR.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aachen Rathaus seen from the south</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Rathaus" title="Aachen Rathaus">Aachen Rathaus</a>, English <b>Aachen City Hall</b> or <b>Aachen Town Hall</b>, dated from 1330,<sup id="cite_ref-WB_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB-23">[21]</a></sup> lies between two central squares, the <i>Markt</i> (marketplace) and the <i>Katschhof</i> (between city hall and cathedral). The coronation hall is on the first floor of the building. Inside you can find five frescoes by the Aachen artist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rethel" title="Alfred Rethel">Alfred Rethel</a> which show legendary scenes from the life of Charlemagne, as well as Charlemagne's signature. Also, precious replicas of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Regalia" title="Imperial Regalia">Imperial Regalia</a> are kept here.<sup id="cite_ref-young2_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-young2-64">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2009, the city hall has been a station on the <i>Route Charlemagne</i>, a tour programme by which historical sights of Aachen are presented to visitors. At the city hall, a museum exhibition explains the history and art of the building and gives a sense of the historical coronation banquets that took place there. A portrait of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> from 1807 by <a href="/wiki/Louis-Andr%C3%A9-Gabriel_Bouchet" title="Louis-André-Gabriel Bouchet">Louis-André-Gabriel Bouchet</a> and one of his wife <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9phine_de_Beauharnais" class="mw-redirect" title="Joséphine de Beauharnais">Joséphine</a> from 1805 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lef%C3%A8vre" title="Robert Lefèvre">Robert Lefèvre</a> are viewable as part of the tour.</p>
<p>As before, the city hall is the seat of the mayor of Aachen and of the city council, and annually the <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne_Prize" title="Charlemagne Prize">Charlemagne Prize</a> is awarded there.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_sights">Other sights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other sights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Grashaus</i>, a late medieval house at the <i>Fischmarkt</i>, is one of the oldest non-religious buildings in downtown Aachen. It hosted the city archive, and before that, the Grashaus was the former city hall until the present building took over this function.</p>
<p>The <i>Elisenbrunnen</i> is one of the most famous sights of Aachen. It is a neo-classical hall covering one of the city's famous fountains. It is just a minute away from the cathedral. Just a few steps in a south-easterly direction lies the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Theater_Aachen" title="Theater Aachen">theatre</a>.</p>
<p>Also of note are two remaining city gates, the <i><a href="/wiki/Ponttor" title="Ponttor">Ponttor</a></i> (Pont gate), half a mile northwest of the cathedral, and the <i>Marschiertor</i> (marching gate), close to the central railway station. There are also a few parts of both medieval city walls left, most of them integrated into more recent buildings, but some others still visible. There are even five towers left, some of which are used for housing.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Church,_Aachen" title="St. Michael's Church, Aachen">St. Michael's Church, Aachen</a> was built as a church of the Aachen Jesuit Collegium in 1628. It is attributed to the Rhine mannerism and a sample of a local Renaissance-architecture. The rich façade remained unfinished until 1891 when the historistic architect Peter Friedrich Peters added to it. The church is a Greek Orthodox church today, but the building is used also for concerts because of its good acoustics.</p>
<p>The Jewish synagogue in Aachen which was destroyed on the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_Broken_Glass" class="mw-redirect" title="Night of Broken Glass">Night of Broken Glass</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">Kristallnacht</a>), 9 November 1938, was reinaugurated on 18 May 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> One of the contributors for the reconstructions of the synagogue was <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Linden" title="Jürgen Linden">Jürgen Linden</a>, the Lord Mayor of Aachen from 1989 to 2009.</p>
<p>There are numerous other notable churches and <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a>, a few remarkable 17th- and 18th-century buildings in the particular <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> style typical of the region, a Jewish synagogue, a collection of statues and monuments, park areas, cemeteries, among others. Among the museums in the town are the <a href="/wiki/Suermondt-Ludwig_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Suermondt-Ludwig Museum">Suermondt-Ludwig Museum</a>, which has a fine sculpture collection and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aachen_Museum_of_the_International_Press&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aachen Museum of the International Press (page does not exist)">Aachen Museum of the International Press</a>, which is dedicated to newspapers from the 16th century to the present.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-68">[63]</a></sup> The area's industrial history is reflected in dozens of 19th- and early 20th-century manufacturing sites in the city.</p>
